Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you cure the dead fetus with what we have said in the division of the medicine and it is not brought out, and you are forced to use operation with your hand, then consider if the woman is of sound virtue and is not with any of the accidents by which she is feared to be harmed.
Then, the woman must be laid on her back on her two, and her head be inclined downwards and her legs be raised.
And the minister should have those two parts tied on the bed so that the body of him does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mouth of the mother with humid oils and with mucilage of althea and fennel and seeds of flax.
And then the midwife anoints her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then she introduces her hand with ease into the mother, and then she searches with her hand in the place where the forceps are in the fetus.
And convenient places in this: therefore, consider if the fetus is prepared that descends on its head, and then you place the forceps in its heels, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or behind the beard, or in the fork of it, or in the places proper of the back, or behind the hypochondria.
But if the descent is on the feet of it, then in the pental, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to have the forceps in the right hand, and to place the curvature of it in that place which is between the fingers of the left hand, and to introduce the hand with ease and place the forceps in one of those places which we have said, until it reaches the left side.
And then place and keep in the opposite position of it another forceps, or the third if it is necessary, so that the traction be equal, and not be extension according to its rectum only.
And be careful to every part, so that the extraction of it be eased as it is done in the case of the eradication of it.
And it is necessary in it that what is between them the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then the midwife anoints some of her fingers with oil and introduces them with care and turns with those that are retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is brought out, then the forceps should be changed into the place which is higher; and do so until the whole fetus is brought out.
But if the hand is outside the other, and it is not possible to reduce it by the constriction of it, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth over it; and then extract it until the whole is brought out, and incise it either at the junction of the hand or in the groin.
And thus it is necessary to do in the other hand and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is constriction in the extraction, or there is water adjusted in the head of it, then it is necessary to introduce between the fingers of the spine and split the head and extract the water, or what describes this with an instrument which is called "alundat", the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is next to this.
And similarly, if the fracture is of great size by nature, and then the bone is inserted, or the bone is pressed together as we have said, then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is pulled out and is constricted by the clavicle, then it is split until the moisture which is in the feet is poured out.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey easily, then you insert the head as soon as it is possible to you.
And if the bowels of the belly are abscesses or are in them hydrops, then you must pierce that until the moisture which is in them runs out.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then the traction of it is lighter and the direction of it to the mouth of the womb is lighter.
But if it is constricted by the belly and the feet, then you must extract that with a cloth which you will have enveloped over your hand, and that you make the belly and the feet until the water which is in those runs out.
But if the other members are stayed and the head returns, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mouth of the womb is obstructed, then she inserts her hand into the perineum, and seeks with that the head, and pulls it with her fingers into the mouth of the womb, and then inserts into it a hook, or two hooks, and pulls it with those.
And if the mouth of the womb is constricted by an abscess, a cautery which happens to the one, then it must not be trebled over it; abandon it, then administer an infusion of moist fatty causes. And administer plasters; and follow the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if possible, it is pricked; and it is administered the water that we have said in the living fetus.
And if that is not possible, then it is incised in parts, and it is extracted.
And you must leave no cause of the second birth.
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer the water that we have said of the cure in the abscesses caused by the one.
And if there occurs a flux of blood, then send them down in astringent waters and administer the other cure.
And I have already seen a woman in whose belly was a dead fetus, and then over that fire another pregnancy, and then a dead fetus is the other. It happened to one a long time ago an abscess in his navel; and it inflated until a fire opened, and it began to throw out pus.
And I was called to the cure of him, and medicated him for a long time, and the plague was not consolidated.
Then I placed on him some causes of plasters of strong traction, and extracted from him a bone, and after passing some days, and extracted another bone; and I was too amazed, as in the belly there were no bones.
And then I thought that they were of the bones of the dead fetus.
Therefore I pursued the plague, and extracted from him more bones, the woman existing in her thousands of dispositions, and already living in such a manner for a long time, correcting of her a little of pus.
And I do not affirm that it is strange, but that in this there is science and help to what the physician artifices with his hand for the cure administers.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of a forceps with which the womb is opened:
This is the form of a stretcher in which the free ends are engaged, to the engagement of which two forceps are at the two extremities of two futz. But however this forceps must be more subtle than the stretcher, and be made either of ivory or of wood.
And its width of each rod is two fingers and the margin of each one is one finger, and the length of each one is one palm and a half; and in the middle place of two rods are two adjustments of that same half rods, of which the length is the majority of one palm and a little more, and the width of each one is two fingers, or a little less.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it may be opened with those when you revolve the two prescribed forceps.
Another form similar to that more subtle and lighter:
It is made of ivory rods or wood according to the resemblance of forceps. But however, at the extremity of it are two additions as well, and the length of those additions is about one palm, and the width of each one is two fingers.
And as you want with those to open the womb, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her feet dangling, open what is between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps engaging according to the quantity that you want of the opening of the mouth of the womb until the midwife makes it so that you want.

Of extraction of the secundina.
When the secundina is retained from the childbirth, then you command the patient to hold her breath, and then make her stir with condition, and strengthen your hand over her mouth and over her nostrils, and if by this it is expelled outside, it is good.
And if not, then you take a bowl and perforate in the cover a hole and place in that herbs boiling the mouth of the mother, as such as wormwood and rue and anise and chamomile and sicados arabic and cinnamon and centaury. All of these herbs or some of them are submerged in water in the bowl, and place that on the fire.
And then place a tube of cane in the mouth of the mother, and keep that inside so that the vapor comes to the cavity of the mother.
And then make her stir as we have said, and the secundina will be expelled with velocity.
But if this cure remains and it is not expelled, then you command the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or in a mixture of alum, and then introduce that into the vagina and caress with that the secundina, and as she has found it, then take it and extend it little by little. But if it is annexed in the womb of the mother, then introduce her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the secundina, extract it little by little according to rectum so that the mother does not feel the traction vehemently. It is necessary that it be changed to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the mouth of the mother is narrow, we have already recounted the cure with stirring and with medication of the bowl with herbs.
But if it is not expelled with all the causes that we have recounted, then guard yourself that you do not tire over it in iteration of extraction, but rather that what is emitted from it to the mouth of the mother lies to the cloth of the woman, and then sterilize that with enguent and tetrafarmacon; because that will make it soften and after a few days it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it is possible, it is elevated to the head and to the stomach, and that is read to the patient; and therefore it is necessary that sub-fumigations and conveniences be administered to that.
But some of the Ancients already are accustomed sub-fumigation of nazitort and dried figs.
The form of instrument with which the woman is sub-fumigated for the retention of menstruation and of the secundina:
It is made of wood or of bronze.
And it is placed the subtle extremity in the vagina and the wide extremity over the fire, and the sub-fumigation is placed over the embers of the fire.
And keep that inside so that the sub-fumigation is consumed and iterate another.
Of cure of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born and the anus of them is not perforated, which already has a subtle and opaque. Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to perforate with her finger that opaque; and if not, perforate it with a sharp spatula, and guard oneself that it does not touch the rectum.
And then place on that wool infused in wine and oil; and then cure it with enguent inside until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is opaque, then place in the hole a tube for many days, and remove it when the child will want to defecate.
And already accidents opacity of the anus by consolidation of plague or of abscess; therefore it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then be cured with what we have said of the cure and of the lead.
Of cure of the fistulas that come from the bowels.
The fistulas that develop in the lower part below the navel and the thickness of which develop near the anus or in the space between the buttocks.
And it is a fistula or a tumor.
And as that swelling is old and flows from the whitish, watery humidity or a subtle pus, and sometimes from these fistulas some penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what is emitted from them by the egress and by the windiness; and sometimes the patient uses the egress, and very often it is emitted from those worms.
And sometimes from these fistulas, some penetrate into the space into the bladder, and into the meat of the vagina; and some of these penetrate into the junction of the thigh and into the notch of the hip.
And in which it is known that a fistula penetrates into the anus and does not penetrate, is that you put your finger inside the anus, and you introduce a subtle probe of lead or iron into the fistula when there is no turpitude.
But if there is turpitude in that, then introduce a subtle probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
And if you do not feel that at all, nor does any cause of evacuation or worms or windiness proceed from the tract, thus as we have said, then you know that it is not penetrating, and then you turn to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the vagina, then the significance of it is the emission of urine from it and prohibition of it so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the junction of the thigh or into the hip and causing pain in the circuit, then the sign of it is that the probe penetrates here if there is no turpitude into the hip, and causing pain in the circuit of the part; and that from it there is pus always; and that it does not help in that medication, nor is it consolidated with an enema.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no cure of all, and the cure of those is the work and vanity of the physician's leaves on which they are based.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected according to what I will tell you, and what I have already said and proved.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his two and elevates his legs into them, the thighs being inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a probe of lead, or of lead if there is turpitude in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.

And therefore if you feel the probe and between you and it is always made of skin or flesh, and it is proper to penetration, then it is not operation in it but according to my trust and of hope.

Of suture of hemorrhoids in which blood runs in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are according to two ways, that they are between the buttock seeming like that of coals, or that those are like the fat of a radish. Of the cure of the plagues.
And I have already said in the division of the cure of the plagues what was convenient to the free, and I say so the cure of those with my and with medicines according to the complement.
Therefore, I say that the plagues are diversified according to what they are made of the plagues and according to the place on which the plagues fall.
And the causes for which many times the plagues are made are like this: by a blow of a rock, or incision of a sword or of a knife, or percussion with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to those of many causes.
But the plagues that are in the places of the body are like this: a plague that falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the arm, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the cure of some plagues so that you pause that reasoning and rule over the other plagues.
And I will begin with the plagues of the head simple, because the remembrance of the composed will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is by a blow of a rock or similar, and the bone has been broken only and it is a great plague and fears about that patient having an abscess, then you cauterize and flebotomize the cephalic as soon as possible, and do not delay this; and extract from that of the blood according to the quantity of the virtue of it, if no other cause prohibits fleubotomia, more especially if the blood that is extracted from the plague is little.
And let the fleubotomia be on the opposite side of the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from coarse foods.
But if from the little (blood) any cause prohibits, then let the food be lessened and do not approach to meat nor to wine.
And pause on the plague, if to it there occurs an abscess cauterize, coat infused in rose oil or in wine in which is steeped.
But if it is certain of an abscess cauterize, then pause on the plague, if it is recent with its blood and the air has not changed that the powder which I will recount a little.
And if the air has already changed that, then pause on ulcers until it is healed.
But if the plague is great or is by incision of a sword and of similar things, and the lips of it are not adjusted by pupils, then adjust those with suture according to that I will recount of the suture of them.
But if the plague is already discovered of the bone and skin, and there is no retention of it but a little suspender, then incise that and at once cure the plague with medicines and restore the flesh about in the place of the bone, and command the patient that he be pescut of foods in which is grosezza: caps and form cooked and similar to them, until it is composed.
And if there is putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that putrid and cast it out, and at once cure that.
But if in these plagues there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow, and it is not incised by medicines, then you inquire about the artery, and if you find that not incised, then you incise that with a spatula, or tie that; and if necessity provokes that what we have said does not confer, then you cauterize until the blood is abscind.
And if these plagues are simple and small, then the cause of them is lighter; in this way that you pulverize upon those this powder, because consolidated that before the air changes those.
And the manner of the powder is this: receive olive oil parted one, of dragon's blood parted two, of caut no-extinct parted three; be all three ground and sifted, and of it be the plague filled, and be constricted by good constriction until well upon that adheres, and be coagulated with the blood.
And at once strengeys that desus with pupils and leyssa that, and do not untie it while it is so that it is not apostemated, because no medicine is eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes do that sole caut, when it is not to you in present enses and dragon's blood.
And already do this same operation in the great plagues when it convenes that it be paused upon those and be streyted, and are recent plagues with their blood, those which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and more especially when it is in the time of summer, then it is not understood to the position of this powder, but rather cure those with what we have said, in this way that you pause upon those some digested enguents, or that you pause upon those powder of the flour of barley with water and honey until they get putrefaction; and at once cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
But if it happens that the plague fracture in bones, and it is small, then you extract it with a tool.
And I will say the cure for skull fractures in that which is venomous.

Of the wounds of the belly.
The indisposition which happens in the belly is either great, or few, or medium.
And small disruption is made difficult by another manner, and if you see that the reduction of them is more difficult, if you do not try the intrusion of them to their place as soon as possible, for that it opens and is thickened for that the entrance of it is made difficult.
And therefore the best incision is the middle one, because in that one the reduction of the hernia is not difficult, just as it is difficult in the two species mentioned before.
And know that the suture of the belly is done in four ways, but two sutures are common that are used in sutures of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures proper are common to others, but however they are more proper in the suture of the belly.
And I recommend to you each one with exposition and explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and there is some cause of the hernia by that, and it is made difficult to reduce that, then it is done in one of two ways: either by the smallness of the incision as we have said, or when the hernia has come inflammation by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then you cover that with a sponge or cloth moistened in tepid water, or that which is squeezed in water with squill, and spikenard, and embrocate the hernia until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes do this with the one which has astringency, and it is better than the water alone in the resolution of the inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation being resolved, then you moisten the hernia with water in which is boiled rue and mallows, for by this it is relieved the inside of it with little cause.
But if the inside of it is excused by this cure, then you widen the incision a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first cure is better, when it is proper that it is not a section, and it is not an occupation to dry it up by necessity and need.
Of the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharp, and the other part is not sharp, and the tip is subtle, and it is not with the subtlety of a spatula, rather it is blunt a little.
And therefore when the wound is amplified and the hernia is outside, then it is covered that its reduction be according to the form of its natural, in its proper place, if it is possible over that, for it is better.
But when the incision is wide, and it is in the lower part of the belly, then it is covered that you make the patient lie on their back, and place their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you place the head and feet higher than the middle parts.
And similarly if the incision is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your understanding and your consideration always in this way that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other.
And thus it is covered that you administer this in the large wounds and in the middle wounds.
And in the small wounds, place that which is prepared by you, and make that it be ministered between your two fingers, the one which has the incision all with its hand and adjusts the lips of it, and then uncover from that to that which sees the suture any cause to another.
And this is the common way of suturing: one that takes the needle, or count of needles according to the quantity of the amplitude of the wound; and then lets it from the end of the incision of the ring finger, and strengthens the needle one, or another that you do not put thread in the two extremities of the needle, with the two extremities of the finger that is left the needle of the parts of the fingers, until you make that penetrate from that part; and you have already added two extremities of the needle and two extremities of the finger, and four orders are made.
And then strengthen with double thread in the circuit of the needle, and sometimes of two parts together, and then add the two lips and the wound by decent addition; and then let go the thickness of the finger and fix another needle in that, and then tie that with thread as it is tied with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if it is necessary with the needles until you have completed the whole constriction of the wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and subtlety, for the subtle needles move lightly into the flesh; and the thick ones are of difficult entrance in the finger, and therefore for that reason they should be medium in subtlety and thickness.
And do not let the needle be placed in the two extremities of the finger properly, so that the flesh is not lightly incised and that the wound be opened before its consolidation.
And do not be prolonged in suturing so that the wound be prohibited from consolidation.
And then cut the extremities of the needles so that they do not prick the patient's sleep; and place on the suture powders expanded of all parts, the ones which have the extremities of the needles; and let those be there until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And these kinds of suturing with needles in this way are most convenient in the small wounds, for it suffices in the suturing of them one needle or two or around those.
But the second suturing is common: and it is that you administer with suturing the four extremities, of the finger and two extremities of the finger, in one go, with a needle in which the thread is enveloped equal in subtlety and thickness.
And when you have pierced these four extremities with the needle, reduce the needle from that same part from which you started, so that the thread is withdrawn from the upper part to the wound, so that the suture is like a suture of cloth with which the cloths are tightened.
And pause between each suture the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture serves men.

Of the cure of a fistula and a wound.

And fistula according to truth is a flawed, hard, white nodule, with which there is no pain and to which the cavity is as the cavity of a bird's egg; and for this reason, some call it a rizatz, that is, a beak.
And it is in some men humid, the one that sweats; and very soon the humidity is cut off in some hours. And some times this humidity is much, and is little, and is thick, and is subtle.

And thus, so that I make you know the cure of a fistula which is made by way of operation with a hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for some of them reach the great veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the head, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to any of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the spine, or to any of the joints composed by composition as if from the hand and the foot; and of these, some give to the proximity of the main member and similar ones.

Of the cure of the sinews and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it makes a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and pus is taken out of it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is above it as if it were fine cloth, and it does not reach the back corruption, and it does not imprint on the bone or on the nerve or on the ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
But however, if the space is prolonged until the corruption presses into some member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the cure of it is that the skin is incised all as if it were turned, and mostly when it is fine and made like cloth, and composes that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin adheres and that that corruption has not achieved for a back consequence, and in the skin has passed through the face, then you cure that with clysters.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the brac that you see is of fetid odor, then you clysterize that with Egyptian medicine, which is of oil and wine and vinegar receiving equal parts, and they are placed in a vessel, and they are heated over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
And then you take from it what is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize with that the sinus, and tighten the orifice of it, and leave the medicine in it for the quantity of two hours.
And then extract that with a sponge; and do this for so many days until the sinus is purified and the stench is removed.
And some times it is clysterized with water of ash, when the medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ash of wood, and put water on it; and then mix that and clysterize with it until you confess that the sinus is then stayed.
And if the patient has no tolerance to the Egyptian medicine nor the water of ash, then clysterize that with water and honey and that it be mixed with vinegar, and clysterize that with wine and honey mixed, because of the property of honey is that it purifies and washes, and the wine makes the sinus adhere, and especially when in the wine there is an excess of astringency and dryness.
And as you do this sometimes and you confess that the sinus is already removed, then clysterize that with water that makes flesh grow in it.
And it is that you take of enguent dyphynicon and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize that with some medicines and other enguents that have the properties which I have already written in the treatise of the enguents.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow in which the cannula of the clyster is not received, then you enlarge it with a small iron little by little, and place in it a poultice wrapped in Egyptian enguent or in basilicon enguent until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the orifice is wide, then you add the lips of it with sutures and leave it in the quantity in which between the clyster its addition.
And if the patient's orifice from which the arm exits is elevated to the upper parts, then dry that in the lowest place that is in him so that the arm runs below, for when the arm is shortened in the depth of the sinus the flesh is in it engendered.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus inside the environment of the lowest bone according to what you want, then perforate it so that you prepare the member with preparation by which the arm runs lightly according to what is to your apparel and that nothing be in it retained any cause of the arm.
But that which is over the orifice of the sinus, which you remove every hour so that what is adjusted in it of the arm may escape. And so that you know if what runs from it of the arm is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And in this way you consider the place of that sinus, if in it the patient feels pain or not, and if there is an abscess in it.
And as your medication is prolonged in this manner, and you see the matter that is thrown over the orifice of the sinus already adheres, or approaches adherence, then the third day you untie it, and when you untie the plaque, then change the cloth for another and renew the enguent.
But if from the sinus a little space away there are subtle viruses, then do not despair of their adherence, for the healing of it approaches, for some men in whom the late generation of flesh in their wounds, and of those it is done the contrary of it.
And if from the sinus after many days it is issued arm undigested, then you know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and is the prolonged cause, then you adjust in drying of the medicines, and if the medicines are humid in their substance and in their virtue, they are dried as a diaphynicon enguent, when there is in it adjusted superfluity of addition of zegi.
And the last one which is cured, is that you take of diaphynicon enguent and it is dissolved in rose oil, and it is over it poured honey in its substance, and then combine that with it, and use of that.
And already it seems in that action, when there is no arm, diaphynicon enguent, honey when it is boiled so that it is thickened, and use of it; or that you take myrrh and aloe and incense, and grind all and pulverize over the honey when it is on the fire, and then line it with water over cloth and strengthen that over the sinus; and line it with the honey which you have boiled so that it is thickened, and pulverize over cloth and strengthen that.
And sometimes it is administered with it long and razitz of lily celestial (and it is lily celestial) and flour of errs and centaurea, and use of those simples or adjust them, grind them and sift and pulverize over the honey which I have recommended; and use of it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this cure, then you know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and already make inquiry in the bones or in the other hard members or to the ligaments; then you cure it with fistular cure, as above is said.
Of the cure of panariti and ingrown toenails and finger incisions and sections.
Panarici is an abscess which arises from the nail of the finger or toe, and often arises in the other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less prized, or it is an abscess of a caut abscess, or it is corrupted, and throws out matter while it corrodes the root of the nail, and often corrupts the whole of it, and often spreads its corruption to the bone until it is to it a fetid odor, and is made the extremity of the finger lamed, and the color of it is made dark.
And therefore when you have that with the water that we have said in the division and do not combine your cure, then cove that you cut with iron all the superfluity that is remaining from the nail and then cauterize the wound with water, for cauterizing much is helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail voids already comprise the flesh to the sides of the finger, and they begin to prick it and let it out, then cove that you place a fine ray below the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and impel it up and cut it with ease, and place over that which remains of the flesh of the medicines comburent corrosive until it be all removed; and then cure this with enguent until it be healed.
But if the corruption has already entered the bone, then cove that you cut the bone that and extract it, for no time is it healed that there is in it the corrupted bone.
And if you see the corruption and decay going into the wound, then you cut it in one of the nuts according to how it is pressed in its memory, and then take care of it until it is healed.
But if the nail is bruised or scraped, and there is pain in that area, then when you first begin to treat the patient, and then dry the nail with a sharp spatula, making a transverse cut from top to bottom, and be careful that the cut does not reach into the flesh that remains under the nail, because you will cause the patient severe pain, and it will lead to the birth of superfluous flesh in that area; and then take care of that area with what will reduce the pain.
But this adjusted flesh, which is born in the hands of some men, is very fleshy, and it is very much in the bones, or very much in the nail.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or the birth of them in some nuts of the fingers. But that which is born in some of the fingers does not hurt, and this one that is born in the joints is very much hurtful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is lighter, and it is that you cut it, following its root with a wide spatula.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore, be careful of the abscess of it.
But of that which is born in the fingers, when you first cut the flesh of it with a round incision into the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws that are convenient for this, and then take care of it until it is healed.
But consolidation, which happens to the fingers either between their many movements or by nature of the man who was born that way, or by consolidation of plague or by burning of fire, or by similar things, then you must cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then put between their cloths of the bandages in oil rubbed so that they are not consolidated lightly, and separate between them, or put between them a lamina of thin lead until it is consolidated as you should.
And similarly if consolidation happens in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to how it is prepared and convenient to the figure of the limb.
Of incisions of vices, they are varicose.
Vices are varicose, and they are veins wrapped thick and full of melancholic superfluities which come in motion in the limbs of the body, and the major arrival of them in the legs, and most in the legs of the travelers and of the laborers and of the carriers.
Therefore, you must administer purgatives of black bile some times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two manners: one is that it be incised and that the black blood be thrown out, and the other manner is that the vein be elevated and all of it be extracted.
The incision of it is according to this manner: first embroil the leg with lukewarm water until the thick blood be dissolved, which is the feculent of the leg, and then strengthen the leg of the patient to the most sovereign part of the groin, and to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one place, or in two, or in three with wide incision, and then elevate the black blood with your hand from the part below the leg and up, and down, until the quantity of blood be extracted that you want and that you see is sufficient, and which will take the virtue of the ailment.
And then tie that up, and command him that he abstain from the foods that engender black bile.
And iterate the cure by phlebotomy when the veins are full and that night to the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you who raise the leg of the patient, if in it there are movements of the veins, and then put it in a bath and embroil the leg of him with lukewarm water until it reddens and the veins are exuberant; or that he be exercised by strong exercise, if the bath is not present to you, until the limb be warmed.
And then cut the vein in the right of the vein by incision in the length or in the back of the finger, or in the lowest part of it of the bone.
And then open the vein with pincers and excise the vein from all parts until it appears to the touch, and it is of the appearance of it that you see that red declining to blackness.
And therefore, as you evacuate and from the vein you see that white which is cord.
And then you put below that the ray until it be elevated and be thrown from the vein, and suspend that with a hook or lion's claw.
And then cut another incision near that one for three fingers, and then excise the vein from the part below the vein until it appears.
And then elevate that and cut that in the back incision of the bone, and then extract that and elevate it until it be extracted by the second incision, and then extract that from the incision that is above that.
And do this until you extract that from the incision that is most sovereign or highest of all the incisions, until all is extracted; and then cut that.
And if it does not obey to be extracted and elevated, then introduce a double strong needle with thread and ligature it and extract it, or introduce it below and turn your hand all around until it is ejected. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult to elevate that part and it becomes harmful to the patient for that night.
And when that whole thing is pulled out, then place on the area of the wound wool soaked in wine and rose oil and un-rose oil, and care for it until it is healed.
And if the vein is contracted having tortuosity in its wrapping at the parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then cover that on all parts of its tortuosity and at the places of its wrapping, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate that whole thing.
And guard yourself that your section does not cut the vein and that you do not harm that, for it will be difficult for you to elevate that; and serve your prudence in that.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not pointed like the hooks nor is it of sharp extremity so that it does not harm the vein; and it is of thick flexibility like a lion, for if it were subtle, it would cut the vein by its subtlety; having in it superfluous thickness as we have said.

On the section for the ailment of nafir.
This ailment is called nafir in our land, and it is pain which comes in certain limbs, and then it is changed from limb to limb.
And I have already seen this as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm, and because it was touched for an hour, I saw that inflammation go as if a worm was pushing towards this, but more lightly than to be feared, as if it were living silver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain went to that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and then it stayed there for an hour.
And then it corrected itself in the other body until I reached the other arm.
And then I recommended to myself that I should turn myself throughout her body as I saw; and it was too marvelous of the permutation of it and the velocity of limb to limb. And I had already seen this before in my own feet in that manner, however, I have seen many who attribute pain to be changed from limb to limb. But I did not see this in the feet as in the woman.
And I did not think it to be anything but that because the woman was old, on whose dry body and exposed veins, in this appearance, that windy permutation was felt.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this retro-cure in those who are delicate and with moist bodies of well-covered veins.
And as you want the cure of it, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not to you apparent at the heel as we have said, then you tighten upon it and leave it lightly and press upon it until that windiness is constricted, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it in your own feet, then you cure it with the shaking of the body and with what you expel windiness, and charms them with foul pills and pills of serapi and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to the species, and according to the places of the body in which they strike, because their diversity is that of those that have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, they are solid; and of those that have three angles, and of those that have tongues; and of those that have shafts.
And of the signs of the  servel is when it strikes in it an arrow, and pierces the bone, and it is taken out of the cyphne which is above the brain, because it happens through it a violent soda, and scotomia, and vertigo, and roaring in the feet, and inflammation and roaring of the tongue, and spasm and mixture of understanding and vomiting of bile, and very much is the speech cut off and the voice is destroyed, and it is taken out of the place of the wound a white humidity resembling powder, and eyes of it resembling the consistency of the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, if it is not outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it strikes the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and feels with that which it is placed in a hard cause, and it is not a void cause; and very much the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the wrist, and blood flows from the wound black, and seizes that coldness of the extremities, and a burning sweat, and syncope; and therefore you know that death descends upon all.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung, are so much blood sputtered from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he expires great spirit, and demands the attraction of cold air.
And if the arrow strikes in the ligament which is in the feet, and the pain then is near the small of the back, and it is made the leg great with vehement pain and with sighs, and it is not bad all the limbs of the muscles.
And if the arrow strikes in the stomach, then very much eyes of the wound feed some undigested food.
And if the arrow strikes in the belly, and it is lodged in it, eyes some wound of the  plagua, or the membrane, or the intestine which is disrupted, and there is no hope in the cure of it the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow enters the bladder, and urine is expelled outside, and there is a process from it to the outside parts, and there is a lot of pain on the patient, then know that it is mortal.
May the other members, as well as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, and arms, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and their likenesses of the limbs, be saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.
And I recount to you what I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify by it in your treatment.
The cause is, that in a man shot an arrow in the lacrimal of his eye at the root of his nose, and you extracted that from him from the other side, leaving the gray of the auricle of him, and he is healed, and did not hurt in the eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who was shot in him in the gray of his eye, leaving the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and could not be obtained from it except for a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion's head in which there were no auricules.
And the Jew healed, and did not hurt in his eye badly.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which was shot in the throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and that is the one that has auricules; and therefore I incised upon it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and therefore I subtiliated with it, and then I extracted it, and the Christian healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who was shot in his belly, and I thought he would die from it; and therefore, since forty days had passed and there was no change in him from his dispositions, I incised upon the arrow and engineered upon it, and he healed, and did not hurt him badly.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow was shot in his back, and the wound was consolidated on him, and since it was about seven years, he extracted the arrow from the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow was shot in her belly, and the wound was consolidated within her, and it was not changed in her dispositions in a year, and the woman did not suffer in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow was shot in his face and the wound was consolidated, and it broke the arrow, not finding much pain here, and many likenesses to this.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the king's princes who was shot in the middle of his nose, and it had already declined to the right side a little, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to the cure of him shortly after the shooting of the arrow, within three days.

And he did not hurt in the place significantly that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on it for many days and did not hurt; and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and it broke the malaute of the extraction of it for a space of many days until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and recounted this to me.

And therefore I adjusted it in the appearance of the wound with that acute medicine until it appeared to me visibly the extremity of the arrow, and passed to me time with that for the space of time within four months.

Of suction and of the qualities of using those.
Suctions are of horns, or of glass, or of wood.
And suction is made in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is suction without scarification.
And this suction that is without scarification is in two ways, whether it is with fire or without fire.
And to the suctions that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood there are fourteen places of the body, of which one in which suctions are placed is the occiput, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle place of the two parts of the neck together; and suction of the chin below the jaw below the mouth; and suction of the two shoulder blades; and suction of the hypochondrium, and it is above the end of the rib; and suction of the two armpits; and suction of the two groins.
And suction does not extract the blood but of the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not catch the virtue through those, as it is caught by phlebotomy; nor is it advisable that suction be made with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.

And of the ventilations alahorcoben the judgment is proper to the judgment to the ventilations of two legs.
And the quality of the position of the ventilations is that you pause the ventilation at the beginning and suck it with a temperate suction, and do not prolong the position of the ventilations, but pause them lightly, and move them carefully, and that the humors come to the place by equal arrival. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place already red and inflate and that the color of the blood appears.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider in the disposition of the body.
Because that man who has tender flesh of rare pores, you should scarify him once and no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And command the ventilator to amplify the scarification and proceed a little, and temper the suction with facility.
And if in the blood there is thickness, then you should scarify twice, because in the first scarification the way of subtilization to the blood and its liquidity is opened. But in the second to the scrutiny acquired of the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then the third scarification should be made so that it can achieve the last.
And finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a little, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we knew that the blood is thick, then we should scarify with a pre-determined scarification.
And the term of scarification in which is the depth of the cut only.
And if that one whose cut is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary that the places of the ventilations be anointed more gently, more open, more resolved; if it is in summer, as is oil of aloes or oil of violets, or oil of two almonds, or oil of quince; and if it is in winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or of sanc, and to those similar.
And if the superfluity is thick, fried, then the oil should be of amaranth, or oil of balsamita, or oil of aloes, or oil of aneti, and to those similar.
But if the ventilator is of wide pores of tender flesh, then it should be prohibited from oil, and it should be given to these the places of the ventilations of their vicinity the ventilation should be washed with waters or with cold water of solatrum, or with water of quince, or with water of bordolaguas, and similar to those.
And that one whose blood is of much humidity, washes his ventilations with strong wine, or with water of myrtle and sumac and similar to those.
And of that one whose superfluities are thick, washes his ventilations with old wine, or with water of sansuc, which is majorana, or of decoction of aneti, or of chamomile, and to those similar.
And that the ventilation should be guarded before bathing; bathing should be administered near the bath for an hour or two, and that sleep near the ventilation.
What is necessary for the ventilator to be regulated before phlebotomy.
You should consider at the beginning: then if the ventilator or the one who is to be fleubotomized is choleric and windy, and has blood acute and inflammation, then it is necessary to precede with cooling causes, such as vinegar, endive, wine, lettuce, and secanabin, and julep, and similar to those.
And pause their foods as much as possible, and birds' meats, and foods seasoned with acids, and similar to them.
And of that one whose complexion is cold, it is necessary to precede with drinking syrup of almiva, or syrup of alsecanabin of seeds, and sumac, and wine very fragrant between old and new; and that he should be commanded to use little drinking and eating, and pause his foods as much as possible and canary and sparrow and small birds and doves.
And that on the day of ventilation and phlebotomy, the drink should be greater than the food.
And that on certain hours to some men, tyriaca alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is muscada, or alcelita should be given before ventilation or phlebotomy or near, so that the principal members be comforted and the blood be subtiliated; and that they should not take that scalfatz.
But the wounds that are incisions should be incisions that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the joints, and the part of the groin, because these members do not suffer incisions on themselves.
And it is not desired for those (wounds) but traction of blood from the limb, just as you place a suction cup on the nipple in the illness of blood flow from the nose; and use it so that the cold suction, hidden from the limb, is dissolved, just as you place it on the belly and the navel; because it thins the limb and warms it and removes the pain because it resolves the suction.
And it is already placed on the boils when they occur in their inflammation or pimple, because by virtue of the strong traction, the pus is drawn out and eradicates the pimple from its place.
And similarly it does when it is placed on the liver and spleen, suction is applied in those enclosed places.
And these incisions are administered with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of tepid water in the illnesses of the spleen.
And this is because the incision should be filled and be larger, so it is tepid water alone or water in which herbs have been boiled to the appropriate water.
Form of incision administered with fire: its width is two fingers, as we have formed it; and the quantity of the preon is the majority of a palm; and it is in the middle of it, in the place that is in the circuit, the majority of it, the track is small according to the quantity that enters into it a needle.
It should be made of a  thick, round, lion-shaped, smooth and  polished piece so that it does not damage the limb with its position on it.
And in the middle of it, a transverse bar of iron or steel is placed, on which a candle is placed with the fire.
And already this incision is larger than the one we have recounted; and the few (incisions) are less than that according to the illnesses and the state of him who uses it.
And the incisions of the infants and emaciated are others than the incisions of the young and of strong bodies.
And the quality of placing this incision with fire on the limb is that it extends the fire with firewood decently in front of a small candle of wax, and place it on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle place of the incision, so that the fire is ignited around the part below so that it does not burn the body of the patient.
And then it is placed on the limb of the patient, and the finger is on the track that we have said, and when we have the incisions and we do not indicate that we show the finger and that it escapes the vapor on that track, then the incision is dissolved immediately.
And then prop the fire according to its manner, and repeat that if it is indicated by that.
But in the incision that is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar nor track, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb alone.
And this is the form of it: And this incision as much as it is larger, it is better, because it can receive a large quantity of water.

Bloodletting is not administered in all members but in the members in which it is not possible to place incisions, because of the smallness of those, such as the ear and gums and similar to them; or in a member devoid of flesh, such as the finger and nose and their similar.
And the quality of using those that have been found leeches that are in the waters of the muddy pools.
And then they are laid the day and night in muddy water until they are famished, and that no cause remains in the belly of those; and then it is evacuated the body at the beginning with phlebotomy and with incisions; and then it is cooled the limb of the patient until it reddens; and then they are placed on it.

Thus begins the chapter of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and the restoration of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Oh, son, know that these foolish doctors and the vulgar boast of this chapter, and those who never regard the books of the Ancients nor read even a letter from them.

First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from those whose preface is necessary before we begin the remembrance of broken and various members, each and every one. Therefore, I say that in the preface of this chapter, we should speak of the summaries of the sermon; and the chapters, whose understanding at the beginning is forced upon the artificers, and they stand upon the truth of those; so it is for you and those who are quick to know or learn this noble art other than yourselves.
And therefore I say that when you happen upon a fracture or dislocation or sprain or wound, then you must, at the beginning, apply yourself to phlebotomy or to alleviate that, or one and the other, if no cause prohibits, as if due to lack of virtue, or the one to whom you attend is a child, or old in decrepitude, or the time is of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And then let him be content in his food of fresh herbs and of birds' meat and of crabs; and forbid him thick meats and fill him with foods and all foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a cauterized abscess, and if you fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen, to which he is accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient must be fed with nutritious foods of nourishment, thick and heavy in which there is viscosity, as such foods made of boiled wheat with water, and rice, and capons, and fish, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh poultry, and thick meat and similar to those, and with this regimen is made coagulation of fracture more lightly and better.
And know that the bones that are broken in men who are in the first childhood and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and unites on the broken bone from every part any cause seeming to glut in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it until it is simultaneously conglutinated and tied together, until they come to the end of fortitude and firmness, as fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the patient's foods, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.
And I come with remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in its distinct chapter from the other.
Many varieties of fracture within itself. And they are diversified the species of fracture of the bone in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, another that happens in stars; and sometimes it is made a fracture according to the length of the bone: and in the fractures of the saddle and stars and additions proceeding and not proceeding, as it is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and it is a fracture with plaque and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture.
And to every species is engendered or torment proper in the restoration of it according to that which will come remembrance of it distinct in its places.
Many signs of fracture of the bone. And by this is known the fracture of the bone: it is the twisting of it, and from that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and so of it when you compress it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is it felt when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a small twisting or fissure, so that you must feel it with your hand and with compression of the whole; however, place upon it of the medicines of which recollection is made at this moment, the one that convenes to the place; and then apply a stricture subtly.
And know that the place when it is arched and not split in two or otherwise that does not happen in it, such as it is remaugued all of its part, then you must begin from your hour to the rectification of it, however, that it happens after a cautery abscess.
And if it happens abscess cautery, then leave it for some days until the abscess ceases; and then bandage it according to whichever manner that you can of lightness and of ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and the sharpening of it is less severe than of the bone in which such a sathagie has happened.
And then strengthen it according to that which will come the recollection of it.

And the quality of the stricture of the tranquil limb is that which I recommend; for that you know that the broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in the form of those.
And of those that are small as the arm and the hand and the foot and similar to those, then you must the envelopment be of very fine cloth.

And then take another bandage and envelop that other time over the broken place with envelopment, and then see to the envelopment at the part farther from the fracture; and be your operation in the stricture of the envelopment and in the mollification of that according to that we have said in the first envelopment below.
