Second Chapter: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for of fractures some is made by percussion of a blow, and the percussion obstructs all the bone within the skull, the which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and for that reason this species is called ascial; or the blow incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision within the rear of it; and this species of fracture is called levitation absolute.
And the plague of these two fractures or is great or little, and of fracture that is contortion or attrition; and cause of it is percussion with a pole or a fall upon a stone, and their similar things.
And this fracture or is penetrating that already approaches the panniculus which is left the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures or is great or little.
And of fracture it is that it is woven in the bone, in the subtlety of the hair small, and for that reason this species is called capillary fracture.
And it is fracture that is made either by a fall or by percussion of a stone and its similar things, that makes enter some lamina of the bone to the parts within, and it is made in the place concavity as it happens to the payroles of eram when it happens to their percussion and it is a part of their entrance to the parts within.
And most of all it is done that in the bodies of bones humid, as if it were heads of infants.
And to all these species fractures sagacious, they are heavy as if stars separated. And I will come with remembrance of all that in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon those and by inquiry of them with tentacles and ostentation of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary species is known because it is discovered upon the bone, and it is absolved, and lined upon it with a tentacle, for the fracture appears black.
In the cure of fractures, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification over pain, as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and roar of those, and resemblances to their accidents in the major dispositions, adopt not the cure.
And if you see accidents non-current, you hope for health, then you begin the cure of it.
The reason is that if it has come to you the afflicted at the beginning as it is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study in the position of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must set the bone before the seventh day,  because that which is left of the bone be corrupted by the pannicol, and that the accidents that we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the pannicol covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and attrition, then you must insert the fractured part and rub it, according to that I will tell you.
And it is that you raise the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, blood flows or abscess caut, then you oppose it with what you have, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and oil rubbed, and make the patient rest until the abscess is settled, and you are sure of blood flow; and then you begin in the circulation of the bone and in its ablation.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you insert the bone with a straight incisor, and this is the shape of it: And then administer another incisor this, a little wider than that; and this is the shape of it:
And then administer more the incisor wider than the second.
And you must have you a number of various incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are at the end of the sharpness of their extremities, and they are of Indian iron or lead.
And use them with facility in percussion on the incisor so that it does not break the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must pierce in the circuit of it before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-profund drills.
And they are not called thus but because those do not pause the term of the bone of the test to that which is near it, because the drill has a round extremity or margin on that which is left the head of it acute seeming to margin or to small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a great number of which as many as convenient to the thickness of each bone,  so that it be to you present in all tests of the brain the extremity of which is acute, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill on the bone and revolve that with your hands until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and pause that which is between all trace the quantity of the thickness of the ray or around that.
And then insert with incisors that which is between each tract of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can on that in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for that, as such as tongs and subtle forceps.
And you must guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the ciph.
And therefore, as you will have circuited the bone perforating, and if it is continued with it, and you will have extracted that, you must raise and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but however, you must have it thinner and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if there remain any pieces of the small bones and splinters, then take that with facility with that which you must of the instruments; and then take care of the wound with liniments and ointments of which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, farther from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec for great lausor; and this is the sermon of it: Necessary is to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of this incisor lent and agile of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is leon and you do not insert any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides forward in length, as you see; that the lenticular part be adjusted to the ciph with the face of it incident acute in the bone.
And then strike with a small mallet on the incision of one side, while cutting all the bones with ease, as it is revolved, and if you are sure that the panniculus does not occur in it, any accident that you fear in any way, and if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Now if there is any cause annexed to the bone of the panniculus in some places of the bone, cut it from it with the tip of the lenticular incision, and deliver it with ease, because it is delivered from its damage and its pain.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and what is separated or taken from the bone is the fascia of it, and remains in it roughness and subtle satagias, then when you remove that roughness and eradicate those satagias with subtle razors, of which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it is possible for you to administer in every place the most convenient of them and the most right of them according to what in which you are engaged in your operation that broken bone and its figure.
And when you administer in your ratio with which razor you cut the bone at the beginning the most blunt of those razors, and then use it closer to the most subtle of it, and do not cease doing so according to continuity until you arrive at administering the most fine and the most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other fractures, petited capillaries and subtle fractures, when you administer care according to what you perceive for the rectification of it.
And it is the cause that it is not to be trusted on all those who have studied in that art. And stand on what we have written has shown in great fractures.
And if the panniculus is cut when you eradicate the bones, then when you take a cloth of it according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth and do this as lightly as you can, so that you do not weigh the cicatrix; and then administer over that ligament late, and do not strain it except for the quantity that the wound has alone.
And leave it with this care for a day or two until you are sure of the abscess caut; and then untie it and then administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, as well as roots of linen and flour of herbs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and similar to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that be pulverized over the wound, as well as they are dried; and universally, administer in it all medicine of property of which it is that it abstergesca and not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and be not in it the dirt of rinsing nor other; nor let you add virulence in it; for when it is adjusted over the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts it and you may it, and it accides upon the patient, then that evil malice.
And it accides in the cicatrix of the head, as from it is modified the bone and most when it is lessened the cure of it, and it is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider then if it accides to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious its whole adopted.
And if it does not accides blackness but from the medicine that is placed upon it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then when you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts .iii., and in those liniments a cloth, and then place it upon the cicatrix; and then cure it with the species of cure that convenes until it is healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but two parts together, or one of those some times, for in it you have two bones, for the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and it does not accides to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then when you introduce the said small one into its nose, and that you equalize that fracture from within with your said finger, and with the said push outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And be done that by you with ease, and guard yourself that no cause by that your operation upon the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach to it, then when it is equalized with the extremity of the ray in which it is little thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And take care of you in the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then about the seventh or tenth of the repose of the abscess caut.
And then introduce into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of such quantity that it fills the track of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the bandages should be soaked in water, and changed every day, but I have not seen it; rather, it is better that the wound be covered with a dressing of fresh egg white with powder of ginger; and then let the bandage remain until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, in place of a bandage, it is inserted into the nose, a piece of cloth wrapped in the skin of a lion; and this is done when the fracture of the nose is very severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And for this cause it is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do bandages.
And if another time, after the cauterization, an abscess occurs, plaster the nose with cerot or with a piece of cloth soaked in strong wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if no abscess occurs after cauterization, then it is necessary to plaster it externally with sesame flour and powdered cinnamon mixed with egg white; and then place a piece of lion's skin over it, and do not touch the nose with any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to cut over them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them; and then close the incision, and cure it with what hardens and consolidates, and with suitable plasters.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then it is necessary to cure it with bandages and administer lead pipes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not accompanied by a plague, it is necessary to observe if the fracture is only on the outside, and not broken in two and curved at the parts inside, then its recognition is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then you introduce your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then introduce the right index finger; and by pushing with that, you bring the inward curvature of the fracture with ease to the outside parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; and in this way, you bring them together.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to administer extension of two parts according to rectitude until it is possible the equation of it.
And if there occurs a concussion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and then place a piece of cerot over the broken jaw, and place a double cloth over it, and place a large splint over the cloth, or a piece of sole equal in length to the jaw; and then tie that bandage according to how the ligation of it is prepared and how you deem the application is suitable, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and place the food of him soft and lion's broth.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in any way, then you take it off and untie it on the third day; and then rectify what has already been altered of it and plaster it with powdered ginger, egg white, or sesame flour, because you will have removed the cerot from it, and place wool over the plaster.
And therefore, as it stands annexed and adjusted that plaster over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not untie it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; for many times this fracture is straightened in three weeks.
And if another time an abscess occurs after cauterization, then administer what we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or splinters, then you must subtilize that you remove those splinters with what you deem suitable for the removal of them from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is strait, then you enlarge it with the fleubotomist according to the quantity of your need.
And then, when you will have removed those splinters, and of those there is no more to be removed, then you close the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the plasters that you deem suitable for it and harden it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
For it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts, one of which does not occur splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that it occurs splinters in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you make two ministers present, so that one holds the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then, with your hands, you must gently mold the fracture into the shape it should be according to the bone, so that there is little eminence or concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then you must place a pad of cloth or wool under the patient's buttocks, and its size should be according to your need; and extend and elevate the shoulder and bind the pad with your hand while you mold the fracture according to the bone.
And if you cannot pull the shoulder extremity outwards, because it has reached the chest, then you must have the patient lie on their neck, and place a cushion of medium size on the muscle of their chest.
And the minister presses the muscle of their chest towards the buttocks while the bone of the shoulder is elevated as it is in its place, and then you correct the fracture and mold it with your hands.
But if you feel pains in the shoulder and they are bad, then you must press on it and extract those pains with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you must make an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have prepared for this purpose, because you had placed under the shoulder the instrument that serves for the chisels; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the shape:
And it is similar to a chisel of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to your need of it, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; that it have two extremities as if it were twice, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plaque which you have incised when you have extracted the pain from the bone is wide, and there is certainty of a pus abscess, then you must adjust the two lips of the plaque with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a pus abscess, then you must cover the plaque with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the fissure of the plaque.
And if an abscess occurs, then you must put a cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it on top of it.
And all, that the tablet is not harmful above the broken bone; and so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or you see the tablet already being remade, then you correct that and strengthen the bandage.
And then place the sleep of the patient on their neck, and place under their buttocks a small cushion at night; so that with this, the supporter of him is raised from the side of him, and therefore the fracture of the shoulder is elevated to the elevation of the muscle; or the arm of him is tied to the neck of him.
And do not untie the bandage unless you see in the place pus or an abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the shoulder is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the side of it and not broken from it but its edges.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and it is not known except by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every manner of ingenuity which is to you possible.
And then place on the place powder of wheat with egg white and linen cloth; and then place a doubled powder of cloth; and then place on it a tablet of fine wood according to the quantity of the spatula whole or at most a little.
But if below the tablet is concave in some of the places of the spatula, then you must mold that concavity with linen cloth, and then raise the tablet according to equality.
And then strengthen above with a long humemate with decent constriction and firmness of the tablet so that it is not remade from its place.
And consider the ligament every day; and every time it is softened, tie it another time and tighten it, and put the splint back if it is displaced from its place.
And lay the patient on his back.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And therefore when this number is complete, then you dissolve the ligament, and that you know for sure that that is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone pes and put behind the codena, then you split it over it, and take it out, and do as we have said in the fracture of the furcula of sadacio of apostema caut if it happens here.
Chapter VII.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle part of it and that is worse; but the extremities of it must be that they are cicaturized and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it when it is broken in the middle of it is that it declines to the parts of it and happens to it great pain, and difficulty in the hale, and cough, and fort leu and vomits blood; and it is declared in it cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be supinated the patient on his back, and place between the spaces of it a cushion; and then press to the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtilia te in the operation of it and in the equation according to all manner that is to this possible and that convenes to this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.
And then place on it plaster and lint and place above the splint of table subtle of gauze or of caligi and of their resemblances in levitat, because that you envelopes that in cloth; and then subtilia te in lian that over the broken bone that no be maugut; and vay am liadura according to retonditat to the back sometimes, and tighten that with strictura decent; and then consider the liadura in every hour, and when it is softened tighten that.
And if necessity provokes the desliament of that you must corrosivity or pruziment, or pain, or apostema that happens in the place, then you coyta and untie that and eradicate the plaster and rectify that which happens by that with manner of curacio of it; and then reduceys the plaster, if times to that the face; and adjust to it strictura until that it be sanat.
Chapter VIII.
Of the restoration of the back when it is broken.
Know that fracture of the back does not catch but in places gross that siegon the back; and of the extremities of them of the part front does not happen to those contricio but because that they are cartilaginous. And cognicio of it is not hidden to the feeling ofves the inquiry of it am les ditz.
And the restoration sia as that enguales the fracture am les ditz according to the manner possible, until that it be engulhat according to that cove, and then plaster and tighten the broken bone am stela, si indigeys of that.
And if the fracture of the back is declining to the parts of it, then happens to the patient pain vehement and punctura, as punctura that happens to that one that has pleureci, and because that the bones put the velament; and happens to it difficulty of hanelit, and cough, and vomit of mot sanc; and this is of difficult curacio.
And already the Antixs so engeniatz in that with motz engientz.
And of lor so that diso: cove that pauses viandas del malaute that engendro imflacio and ventositat, for that the belly sia imflat and sia extendut, and the fracture sia impellida to the parts offora.
But nos avoreyssem aquo for that it no sia causa leugiera to generate apostema caut, si no accideys; and if already accideys, then helps ad aquo.
And diyssero alcus of lor that it sia pausada desus ventoza and then that sucque am fortitut; and es semblant a ratiocinacio, mays empero es temut that the ventoza atire las superfluitatz al loc per la debilitat.
And diyssero alcus of lor: cove that the loc sia cubert am lana that ja es submersa in oli caut, and sian pausatz pulvils entre las costas entro that sia umplida, that sia the liadura engual when envolopas that segon retonditat; and aprop sia curat le malaute am curacio de pleuresi of vianda and of medicina.
And if cause vehement constrenh the malaute the qual no sufferta, and the bones punh the velament am punctura ledent, temem sobre the malaute, then cove that fendam sobre the loc, descubram de la costa trencada, and aprop pausem desobre that instrument that conserve the ciphac, the rememoracio of the qual es precedida, and that inscidam the os am facilitat, and extraguam that.
And aprop that we adjust the doas labias of the plagua, si es gran, am cozidura, and curem that am emplastres entro that it sia sanada.
And if an abscess occurs, then cover it and put powder in rose oil and place it on the area, and let the patient be cured with what covers the abscess.
And let him be laid back on the mat on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter IX.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the vertebra of the back and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and just as much in their articulation, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if it will heal or not, consider this: if sometimes his hands are already softened and stunned and dead and cannot move them or extend them or tighten them, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, he feels no pain, then you know that it is not healing, and in the worst of dispositions it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels pain from constriction with the fingers and puncture, then you know that the marrow of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with healing.
And if you see in the vertebrae of the neck the appearance of this and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, it happens to those who are affected in the hands, and when you put your hand on his back, there is a feeling of wind and expulsion of his will, and sometimes he is laid back on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work on his cure.
But if you do not see any cause of this, the cause is better and less severe.
And the cure of him is that you perforate the seat of the abscess with a cautery and place rose oil on the fractured vertebra, or with poultices of herbs. Place that on it several times a day until the abscess is cauterized.
And then place one of the comforting and existing poultices, and strengthen it on that with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he does not sleep on the part with which he feels pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that the articulation of the bone is shattered or some cause that is already separated from it, then cover that with a bandage and that you keep that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then treat that with some flesh of the intestines, until it is healed.
But if the back of the bone is broken, and it is the end of the spine, then cover that with a bandage and that you keep that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then treat that with some flesh of the intestines, until it is healed.
And if you feel shattered fragments in it, then cover that, and keep that, and cure the wound with the cure that has preceded until it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the restoration of the shoulder.
The bones of the shoulders are often broken, and if they break, the fracture of them is nothing but a communication at the extremities of them and they are incised in the length and incline to the parts around them; and it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the shoulder is stunned when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it so that it is over the fracture as is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of it, only then do you adjust it as it is apparent to you by equation, adjusting until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long or that which already inclines to the parts below, then have the patient lie on his belly until it is prepared to you the restoration of that fracture. And then when you have adjusted it, place on it a poultice, and then place on that a bandage of fus or of bandage, and strengthen by constriction over which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the bandage.
And adjust the concavity with what fills that until that presses the constriction according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if an abscess occurs, then you cease from extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is cauterized, and place on it what sits that according to what has preceded; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as you cover.
And if there happen to be bone fragments or they are comminuted, then you treat them.
Chapter XI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the humerus.
Humerus is that which is between the shoulder and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a stick of medium thickness according to this form:
And there are bands at two extremities, and then it is suspended from the elevated place.
And follow the patient on a chair; and then throw out his broken arm onto the stick until it reaches the tip of it in the middle place of which is annexed the curvature of the stick.
And then, gently suspend something heavy above it, or extend the minister to the sides; and then, the physician, with his own hands, sets the fracture together, while the fracture returns according to its place.
And the other way is that the patient be laid on his neck and suspend the hand of his neck with a ligament; and then command two ministers that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the pus below him; and each of them extend to his side.
And if you want the extension to be more strong, then you strengthen over the fracture with a ligament and over that with another ligament, and each of the two ministers extend to his two sides.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it should be placed half of the ligament on the  citillic and the other behind the fracture of the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then it should be placed the ligament on that location and on that joint.
Bind that with ease and work, and as soon as it is bound according to its place and applied for good application, then strengthen that if it does not accidentally cause an abscess caut.
But if an abscess caut occurs, then loosen the stricture within the seventh day, and place upon it wool cloth soaked in wine vinegar and rose oil until it rests the abscess, and then you strengthen that.
And the way of strengthening that is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm upon that support, and place your hand open upon his muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm be in place of stars, if it does not prohibit anything from the broken bone.
And then use of stars.
And it is that you place upon the fracture that star that is more wide and more strong of the other stars; and place between each star the width of the finger, and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers of all part.
And then strengthen over the stars with the stricture which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your stricture over the place of the fracture be more straight. And as much as the fracture is longer, the stricture is smaller.
And therefore, if you see the situation of the stars and the stricture as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear an abscess caut, then you loosen the stricture and the stars within the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that nothing happens in the place, proximity or inflammation; or it is prohibited the food to arrive at the limb by disposition of superfluity of stricture; and therefore rectify everything according to what we have said.
But if you are over confidence that nothing will happen from that, then do not untie the ligature until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hands be on his stomach.
And place upon the support a full cushion of wool binding, and consider in all hours of the night or of the day that the figure of the broken limb be not loosened nor dissolved, or that the ligament be softened, therefore rectify that all with your virtue.
And place the patient's food according to the order which we have previously said, in such a way that the food be subtle at the beginning until it is begun to strengthen; and then it should be thickened for him.
Because of custom of the support and the bed of him is that they be strengthened in forty days; and then it should be loosened and be administered a bath and be medicated with poultices those which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid raw, then you do not untie from that the ligament and the stars within a day or within two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forearms; of which one is small, and that is the one that  touches the little finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small finger, from the part further down.
And often the major forearm is broken alone, or the small one, and often they are broken together.
And when the small forearm is broken above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when the forearm of it below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone which is broken is the small forearm above, then it is convenient to the physician, it behoves the restoration of it, that place small extension with ease until that be bound.
But if the large forearm is that which is broken, then it should place extension more vehement.
And if the two forearms are together broken, then it should you place the extensure more strong.
And how you support the figure of the hand, you must restore it and the extension of it extended over a cushion, and the wrist of the hand to the upper parts, more than all the others said, and the said auricular more low of the others said, and the patient be centered on a fallen chair.
And there be a cushion in front of him in the height or upright of him, so that the patient does not bend work.
And then the minister extends the arm of the farther part with his hand or ligament, and another minister extends upwards seemingly.
And then the doctor aligns the bones as he reduces that according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the facture of the bones so splints, then you perforate to reduce all pieces in their place with your virtue.
And if there appears in him a separated splint that punishes the bone and is not at your hope in the restoration of him, then you end upon him and hold that according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a plague, you have already made to this chapter singular, and therefore take the cure of that.
And if it happens abscess caut at the beginning of your restoration, then you line a cloth with cerot made of rose oil and white wax, and be medium between specificity and subtlety, and strengthen cloth upon that with subtle structure until the abscess is ceded; and then remove the cerot and place plaster prepared of powder of flour with clayre of egg, and then place stars; and be the star which is placed upon that fracture more wide and more strong.
And know that the number of the stars of the arm so six in the major dispositons, or be the fracture in one focus or in two foci together.
And then place your structure upon the place of the fracture most strong and most vehement, and when more it is the stricture to the upper parts of the fracture or to the lower parts of it, place the stricture a little more soft according to that it is preceded the remembrance of it in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are enveloped upon the fracture be soft cloths humid, and not be hard nor rough, and the thread with which it is strengthened be of its proper medium between subtle and gross, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and the ligament about some days. And if there happens any cause of which rectification is necessary, as you prudently the one which happens to the limb, and then cover that you embroil the limb with warm water until that prudency ceases.
And let the limb not constrained until it rests, and then return the structure.
And if the structure already is softened and the bone already is remaugut and seems to those, then rectify all that with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited to arrive to the limb by superfluity of structure, for then cover that you soften that a little and let it for some days until it runs to that the nourishment, and then strengthen that.
But if to the patient there happens no cause of those that we have said, then cover that it not be dislocated within about xx days or around those.
And then be suspended the hand of the patient by its virtue of movement agitatius, and make that sleep upon its two.
And know that this fracture is restored in xxx days or in xxxii days, or fort less it is restored in xxviii days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complexities and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter .xiii.
Of the restoration of fracture of the pinkie of the hand and of the fingers.
Know that to the pinkie of the hand and to the knuckles of the fingers tart it ought fracture, and there happens to them not attrition many times.
And when there happens to the hand fracture or attrition, then cover that the patient follow sitting and before him lies centered according to equality, and then place the hand of him upon that extended.
And then the minister extends the broken bones and the doctor aligns those until they are conjoined with good conjunction.
And then cover that you place plaster and estopa, if there happens abscess caut; and then place stars above according to the quantity of the place, and you already have enveloped that in cloth of it.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers of the palm, and then make semblance of sphere of cloth, and command to the patient that strengthens that with his palm fractured; then be strengthened with long cloth.
And be the star of leather with cloth in which it is levitated, so that the leather be applied to the palm; and strengthen that according to that cover.
But if the fracture is to the parts outside, then cover that you place above star and another star of the farther part in the palm, so that the hand be open and straight; and then place the structure, as it is revolved the hand to the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if there happens fracture to some of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the finger placed, be aligned according to that cover and then be strengthened with the palm and if it wants, place to it little star straight so that it be elevated the fracture and not be mauguda.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index, or in the ear, or in the ring finger, then it should be bandaged and tied with the same finger that it is; or all should be tied according to continuity, and this is better; or rest on that small splint, as we have said in the hand.
And consider, in your restoration, and about that, the disposition of cautery abscess and oppose — in your operation — with that which covers when it happens any cause of those, according to that is iterated the narration of it.
Chapter XIV.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh, when it is broken, is felt according to how it is converted to the front and the most sovereign part.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament is left below the fracture, and the patient is prone on their belly and face.
And then the minister extends all the ligament to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is towards the root of the thigh, then the ligament is pulled down to the part of the pelvis, so that the extension comes in its, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is towards the pelvis, then the ligament is made towards the pelvis, and the extension is to the parts below.
And then the physician joins the bone with both its hands until it is reduced according to the natural likeness and the bone is joined by good connection; then it is covered with plaster and strapping, if it does not happen to the limb abscess. But if it happens to it abscess, then you leave it for some days until the abscess caut is yielded, and then return to your cure.
The strapping of it is done in such a way: that a hard bandage is wrapped over the fracture, and doubled two or three times, and the superfluity of it is removed; and then the leg is wrapped until it reaches the thigh and the root of the thigh.
And introduce a long splint between the thigh and the leg, from the part of the pelvis to the lower parts, and the extremities of the splint come from the most sovereign part of both parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh what superfluity of the bandage.
And then place on the thigh in the place of that fracture splints, and place from those one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the strapping is coagulated.
And then begin with strong strapping of the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as much as you go away with the ligament from the place of the fracture, your strapping is less and lighter and softer.
And then extend the two extremities of the splint to the lower parts until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both extremities of the splints on the other part so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
And then leave the strapping on it, so that it does not happen to the limb corrosion or abscess caut or inflammation and similar to those; and if it happens any cause of those, then count you yourself from it, and rectify what happens all again, according to that we have said many times.
But if in the bone is heavy of the bone that you put, then cover that according to what is possible to you; and if not, then place on it and remove that, and care with that of which is preceded the remembrance until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened with another thing that is adjusted to it splints, as we have said in the adjutori and in the arm.
But however in the restoration of it in such a manner made, do not happen to the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, other than that which is applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he limp from the one of which it is always.
And know that the thigh is tightened in 40 days or a few months or a few less, and all that is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the hip bone.
Know that fracture of the hip bone is hard to endure, but it happens to it many times attrition.
But if it happens to it fracture, then it is not but fissure or crack in its parts, and that is with a plague or a few plagues, and over that all is state with the feeling.
And the restoration of it is in such a way that what is broken of its parts is joined with the fingers until it is aggregated and joined according to what is possible by equation and facility and fabrication.
And then place plaster and place on it a round splint, if you need it; and tighten above with strapping to what is convenient.
And then consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in the other fractures, as abscess caut and similar to those in such a way that it opposes to all accidents to what is convenient until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick, and it is named by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and it is named fibula.
And it happens to some of the kinds of fractures that happen to both bones of the arm, and for that reason the restoration of it is done as well as the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if two bones are broken together, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is converted to the parts in front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it bends that one, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.
And then administer extension and reduction and ligation of the splints according to that same manner.
However, more over, if the fracture of the leg is large having many stages, that the extension be less and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue.
And in the leg there is some cause of operation on the arm, and it is when you align the splints and that completes all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood of the pine which is placed below the causes which are placed in their sockets, and they are made from branches of palm; some of those to which is small thickness, and no be of the branches, and be the length of them according to the length of the leg, and from the knee to the furthest.

And then tie the two splints in three places of two extremities and to the middle place, because with this structure the leg is prohibited from bending to the right and left part, and it stands for good station.
And already is administered a splint of wood according to the length of the leg; and be placed in it the leg so that it conserve that of movement.
And most importantly that you do this in fracture, and when with that is properly plague.
And then consider in each day the leg and study with that by posterior study, and you guard it from abscess and from inflammation or other causes similar; and when it happens any cause of those, then oppose yourself to it with what you must within that it be healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the heel bone and of the bones of the foot and of the toes.

Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the hip bone of the woman and of the bone of the pubis and of the groin of the man.

And then place pillows on their back, and when he wants to urinate, move the buttocks with ease and then urine, and reduce and return that according to the manner in which you placed it at the beginning, and then return to his first pillow.

Chapter XIX.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when it is with a plague.
It is convenient for him to whom happens a fracture with plague, and especially if the bone is large as is the bone of the hip or of the shoulder, or similar to that, that you take care and flebotomize it as soon as the conditions of flebotomy to you or testify as we have said above.

But if the broken bone is eminent over the uncovered hip, then you must take care to perforate reduce it and to equalize it with your hands as easily as possible and with little extension.

And this is the form of it:

Because it must be placed the extremity of it to ascend over the extremity of the bone eminent.

And then cure the plague if to you is seen, and if not leave that.
Chapter .xx.
Of the cure of nodacio that happens in the vestiges of some fracture.
Many times happens this nodacio in the vestiges of the healing of fracture, and mostly that is near the joints, and is marred by it the figure of the member; and very soon prohibits the member of its natural operation.
Therefore consider if the nodacio is recent and administer in that medicinas that have stipticated as well as aloen, and incense, and mira, and sercacollo, and acrassia, and similar to those; in this way that you take of that some or all, and comfesiys those with wine stiptic, or with album of hen, or with wine agre, and place that on the nodacio in estopa, and constrict those upon it for good constriction.

Chapter .xxi.

Chapter .xxii.
Of cure of the bones broken when they are restored tortuously and prohibits the operation of them of that which cove.

Chapter XXIII.
Of the speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from its place, so that movement is prohibited, and the figure of the limb is distorted, and it brings upon the patient pains and great injuries.
Therefore, when an accident happens to some dislocation, then it should be immediately reduced, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed, the place is inflamed and very much swollen with water, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be bad or stretched in the hour of the inflammation, for many times it brings spasms and terrible pains.
But when this happens, then you should take care of the patient's inflammation, and let it rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be soaked in lukewarm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be cured with water of which the remembrance will come to its place.
And you have already ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the part of the body most sovereign to the part most below.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So dislocated the two mandibles if not tarried; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: that or they are remaugged from their places by little movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by entire dislocation and complicated, until they are softened at the junction, until the saliva of the patient flows and that it cannot hold it, nor can it close its mouth, and the tongue of it be enveloped in its word.
But when the dislocation of it is little, then in the greatest dispositions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complicated, then it should be administered reduction of it gently, and not be delayed at all.
And that the minister has the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts the tip of one of his hands into the root of the dislocation of the lower jaw from the inside of his mouth, if the dislocation is of one part; or that he inserts both of his hands together, if the dislocation is of both parts, and the others of his hands are outside with which he grasps; and commands the patient to cease his jaws and allow those to go from any part; and the doctor grasps the mandible and pushes the mandible until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and most especially if both mandibles are dislocated at the same time, then administer vaporization with lukewarm water and oil, until a gentle reduction of both is made and the reduction of both is not delayed at all, as we have said.
And therefore, as they return and are grasped, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then place over them powders of cloth with cerot, and it be made of wax and olive oil rubbed; and then be tied with ease with soft linens.
And let the patient's sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or to the left; and do not allow him to chew for any cause; having his food be forbidden lean, until when the pains are remaugged and the jaws are well-knit, then allow him to chew as much as he wants.
And that water be administered with ease, and do not work over the opening of the mouth of the patient for eating and drinking and spitting, until the mandible is well-knit and healed.
And if it is difficult the reduction of two mandibles when they are dislocated at any hour and are not converted to their places, then many times accidents happen fevers and assiduous soda, and very much is alarmed the belly of the patient, and very much and vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is pernicious; and many times dies he to whom this happens in 10 days.

Chapter .xxvi.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three manners, of which one is that it is dislocated at the part of the thigh to the part below.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the part above the muscle, and that is done quickly; and it is not dislocated at the parts behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the parts in front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and outside, at the parts below the thigh, and mostly in those in which the flesh is little, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is brought outside with difficulty.
And often accidents happen to some men by blow or fall, and the muscle of them is suppurated by a boil, and it is cured by that which has already been dislocated.
Therefore, when you see that, while you are over-seeing it, and then proceed in its cure.

If it is dislocated below the feet or above, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.

Chapter .xxvii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the hip.
Know that the joint of the hip is dislocated with difficulty and seemingly returns with difficulty; and that is dislocated to all parts, and mostly to the part in front to the part behind, and the dislocation of it is not avoided to you because it is caught below the vein or below the touch according to whichever figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated hip with the its, that is to you declared manifestly, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch with that the muscle.

Corruption has already arrived on the inside of the tooth while it is being rinsed.

Chapter XXVIII.
Of the cure of dislocation of the shoulder, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
Shoulder dislocation some times is dislocated, and the reduction of the dislocation of it is lighter, and the contrary is of the other joints, however, as long as it is speed in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place is festered or that happens in it fester.
And the reduction of the dislocation of it is that you place the shoulder joint of the patient on a table and the minister extends the arm of that, and the physician places his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes that until it returns to its place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm is already placed in the belly of the hand, then the patient places the back of his hand on a table, despite the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is previous to the back of the hand, then the position of the hand of that intrinsically on the table, so that the hand of the physician lays on that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then strain that with a poultice, softening the festering and leave that; and do not repeat this, because if not, do not suffer nor can you over the reduction of it more than passing days.
However, the joint remains according to its tortuosity; and do not give the patient any cause, unless the hand is softened and cannot strain any cause; then you know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then it is not good in it unless it is strained with a cautery near the place of it and strongly do that confer any cause.
Therefore, when the shoulder is returned, then place on it the poultice which have been recommended, and let it be left and strained for five days; and then let it be removed and let it be left and the hand of it be exercised.
However, if the movement of it is stayed and happens in it any gravity, then gently that with hot water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the said.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated from those to the back of the palm or to the belly of it, then strain the finger and push the dislocation of it with your fingers, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until straight it is straightened in that day.
And when it will be night, tie that according to that narration, and do not cease to untie that in the day and exercise with movement, and tie that in the night; and do this for some days until it is straight.
And do the same with this, if it is dislocated to the belly of the hand, and tie that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When there happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck a dislocation complicated, and so remauguts motz vertebrae from their places, then in that there is no cure, because death takes hold of the patient.
And sign of this is that the digestion of the patient is out-ridden of its will, and cannot retain that; and many times are softened some members of it or both legs of it or both arms of it, or one of those; or is remaugut a vertebra from its place, because many times is remaugut and many times is made the removal of those to four parts.
Therefore that is already remaugut to the parts behind and is called gelbozitat.
However, the cure of it is that then consider if the gelbozitat happens of puerissia, because then in that there is no cure nor of that there is no healing of all.
However, in that which happens by accident or by percussion or similar to those, already said the Ancients the ways of cure with sermons prolixs, of which the most returns with prophecy; but I already and shortened of that of which the little excuse of the too much of it, for the my better proximity to the better understanding exposition, with which I have this explained and formed ex instrument divers of that with which I will show this and expose that.
And of these, only that one is cured which happens in the back properly with what I recommend.
And that the patient be extended on their back, with their legs raised close to the wall, and their lower limbs be moistened so that the feet of the patient are not lifted.
And then a straight stick be placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the leg near the head of it, and another stick in the opposite direction of the feet at the other end of the leg.
And the minister holds the stick, and it is not fixed in the ground; and another minister holds the other one according to that form.
And then wrap a cloth around the head of the patient and below the shoulders, firmly; and extend the limb to the stick which is above the head, and tie it there; and then tighten the other ligament on the thighs of the patient, and below the knees, and then adjust all the ligaments and tie them to the other stick which is below the feet.
And then the minister carefully extends the ligament to the part of the stick, and do not be removed the two sticks and the ligaments from their places in which they are fixed, but however do not be fixed as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength while returning, or that which is placed on that a table, and then that he adjusts on the table his feet while returning.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table of which the width or length is opposite of three spans, and carve in the wall of which we have said that it is near the patient a place in which enters the end of the table. And then place the majority of the table on the spine, the doctor places his feet on the other end, and tightens well until the spine is shortened and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a turn which is revolved with the hand; and it is that you fix in the ground above the head of the patient in the rear of the leg two sticks, the length of which is of one arm, and above the head of it in the rear of the leg two sticks as well; and let the length that is between each stick be a palm, and already is made in each stick a trough in which runs the turn, and each one be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And let there be also a round stick in which is the turn, and it is revolved in the two troughs of the two sticks.
And in the extremity of it there is a trough in which is formed the stick, the length of which is a palm with that which is revolved; and in the two other sticks is similar to that.
And then tighten the ligaments that you have straightened in the feet of the patient in the trough which is above the head of it, and the ligaments that you have straightened in the thighs of the patient in the turn which is below the feet.
And then above each turn and there be a minister that twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor straightens the spine according to what we have said.
And as soon as the spine is returned, and the place is straightened, then cover with a paste made of dried albumen of egg, and then with cotton wool; and then place on the paste a board of the table of which the width is of three fingers or around those, and the length of it is as much as it stretches the place of the swelling, and on the part of the spine it lays.
And then tie it with the ligament which we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment while it is healing.
And if there remain any swelling in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary that medicines be administered which soften and cleanse with the administration of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already is administered in that place a plate of lead.
And some times there happens swelling in the rear of the vertebrae of the two, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells for which it is not necessary that it be done recklessly to it with this medicine, because very strong it will make you happen death.
Chapter XXXI.
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle ought not to be dislocated only, and it does not happen to those that happen to the other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, because it is dislocated to the front parts, or to the back parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the front parts, and few to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it to the front parts is that, when you adjust the thigh of the healthy leg with the leg, it is longer and the knee is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his leg above the knee, and it is the place that the knee is swollen with pus, so that the head of the thigh has already arrived here.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs is that the parts of him are contrary to this accident.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs in the front parts is that he expands his leg according to the fulfillment, but however, he does not double that pain that is in the thigh, and if he tries to go and cannot do so above, the front parts; and the urine of him is retained and the groin of him is inflamed, and he must walk of him on the crutch.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs in the rear parts is that he does not expand the thigh, nor can he bend that front which he bends the groin, and the leg of him is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is that place.
But the reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, therefore, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that it is already fixed with that having, then you administer reduction of it and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and therefore no cove that in that be made presumption.
But in the one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation, then you take hold of that which you want to return the joint and extend that to the front and rear parts, and move that to the right and left, because it strongly returns and does not need other cure.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the leg from the farthest part, or with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the thigh; and another minister extends the hand of that below so that he puts his hands below the thighs of him; and then tighten with a bandage on the root of the thigh, and hold another minister the extremity of the bandage.
And that extension of him or from the front part to the groin to the fork, or from the rear part to the part of the loin.
And that extension of all those in one go while the patient is elevated with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to all species that we will say of the proper cure.
In the reduction of it proper, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the front parts, it is necessary that the patient be laid on his side; and then place the bandages on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and then extend the bandage from the part of the groin to the highest part of the fork.
And then another minister strongly presses the arms of him, and grasps the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extends to the front parts by vehement extension, so that it returns to its place.
And this species is of lighter cure with which this limb is reduced than the others.
But if it is excused on you and you do not obey the entrance with this species of cure of all, then it must be tied both feet of the patient together with a strong, light bandage, on both the legs and both thighs.
And the length of each of its parts is the quantity of four digits, and the leg of the patient extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then the patient be suspended on the top of the pole, and that it be in the middle; and the length of him from the ground is the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to pull on the head of the thigh, and let another young man suspend with the patient, and the other young man pull the thigh with strength, so that the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is in the front part, it is necessary that the patient lie on his stomach as we have recommended in the having gelbosity; and be tightened the bandage on the leg of the patient properly and on his feet.
And then two poles be placed, one to the feet of him, and the other to the head; and then be placed poles adjusted in the middle of the groin placed on which wet cloths be enveloped, so that the patient be not damaged for that the pole be between the thighs of him, for that the extension to the rear parts be not pulled.
And then each minister extends to his part, and the physician with his hand engulates the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration about groin; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the groin of the patient, and then he pulls that with the other hand, and with that pulling, he pauses that extended to the rear parts to the part of the thigh.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then it is necessary that the patient be not extended to the rear parts, and it is elevated above the ground; it is necessary that it be placed on a hard cause, as it is necessary that the eyssament if the width of him is dislocated in the front parts, according to what we have said of the jasement of him on two widths; and it is on his face and the bandages are extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And a compress should be administered with a table over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, as the reduction is complicated according to what is needed — and the sign of the reduction of it is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient extends his leg and expands that space, then you know that the limb according to what is needed is returned — and then you adjust two cushions and place a poultice and extend with a bandage so that the width is not bad in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or for four.
And then untie the ligament and the poultice, and compare one leg to the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and therefore let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then you return and plaster that, and extend that according to your first structure, and let that be for three days.
And then untie that, and make that person delay walking for a while, until it is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, which is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inside parts, and to the parts in front to the parts behind; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply his leg to the thigh; then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of it is that you make the patient straight, so that he already extends his leg, if he can above that; adhere your two to the face of him, and place his foot between your feet; and then make adhere the knee of him to your palms, and keep those between your fingers on the knee of him with strength, and another minister extends his leg while the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction of it is that his leg adheres to the thigh with lightness without labor.
And then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then tie those together with a bandage for three days or for four, and then untie those.
And do not use the patient for walking for some days, until it is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the hernia preceding narration.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle some times is sprained by slight movement, and some times is dislocated according to complement, and the dislocation of it is made either to the inside parts or to the outside parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the ankle inflated proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the sprain of it is of slight reduction, and is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be equalized while it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to complement, you should make the patient straight, and have that one minister strong and valiant from the back of the two in the middle place of him.
And then you with your right hand the foot of the most sovereign part, and with your left hand from the part most below of the foot in the place of the ankle.
And then you pull the foot to you with your right hand, and with the left for two times, and then extend with the left; and push the foot of the foot with the right towards the leg so that the leg works.
And do this two times according to what we have counted.
And then push the foot of the foot to the leg the third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore if it returns in one time or in two according to this manner, and if you see the foot equalized, it is good; and if not, then on it iterate the operation, for that it returns.
But if to you is forbidden the reduction of it with that which we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a piece of wood on the ground firm, and that it lies between the thighs of him, and you already have enveloped over him cloth so that it does not scratch the patient.
And then the minister of him holds his thighs, and another minister extends the leg with his hands or with the ligament that lies on the collar of the foot; and then extend that each minister against extension of his part, and the piece of wood be extant between the thighs of the patient retaining that, so that the body of him not be pulled to the parts below towards his extension.
And then the physician equalizes the dislocation with his hands, and another minister holds the healthy thigh to the parts below, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore as the dislocation returns and is declared to you the health of the reduction of it, then place plaster and extend that with ligaments strongly, and tie the foot with ligaments to the leg.
And be careful that you avoid the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the ligament on that side is not too strong and does not damage it strongly.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the ligament is softened, withdraw it.
And on the third or fourth day, untie it, and forbid the patient from moving for 40 days. But if he is forced to go before that time, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted on him, and therefore that it will not receive healing.
And if an abscess occurs to him, then you must administer in the treatment of it the water which we described earlier in another place of healing and ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocations of the toes.
Be careful that the one who is dislocated be wrapped with little extension of his work; and that which is not difficult, raise it lightly.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the foot, then you must make the patient place his foot on a level surface of the earth or on a table, and standing thus as if he were walking.
And then you stand on his feet, and place your foot on what you feel from the joints, and then press that with your foot with virtue until it returns, and that you see it already healed and does not appear in the place eminence.
And then place under the foot of him a table that supports the whole foot, which has two ends, and then strengthen it with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its return is assured.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the kinds of dislocations that are made with plague or with fracture or with any of both.
When any of those causes occur, and you engage in the cure of it and in its restoration, many times death occurs.
And for this reason, you must not presume over the cure of it, but only he who is wise in the art, of long usage, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not hasty nor presumptuous; and that he uses in the beginning of medicines that cause abscesses cauteously only, and leaves the patient to the divine judgment, but to him whom you hope for salvation from destruction, with the alleviation of the illness, and in which you appear any hope. And therefore, strive as soon as possible to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a cauterized abscess.
And therefore, if the limb returns as we want, then administer the regimen which causes a cauterized abscess and cures the plague with the water that convenes to it of the dried herbs.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the broken bone fragments are separated, then you must pierce the stem of them, and do in that what we have said in the simple illnesses of this, which is preceded in those places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the treatment in the ways of fear according to what has preceded me in your preaching; for this is better to you, you owe your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, of which God be the benefit. Amen.
